# **Abstract**

The power conversion efficiency (PCE) of single-junction perovskite solar cells (PSCs) has experienced unprecedented growth over the past fifteen years, rising from 3.8% in 2009 to 27.4% in 2025\. However, this trajectory now approaches the fundamental Shockley-Queisser (SQ) thermodynamic limit of 33.7% for single-junction architectures, leaving diminishing room for further improvement through conventional absorber optimization alone. This dissertation addresses the critical challenge of transcending this efficiency plateau through a fundamentally new paradigm: the integration of bimetallic gold-silver (Au/Ag) plasmonic nanoparticles (NPs) into the perovskite absorber layer via advanced nanostructured surface engineering.  
The research methodology integrates three complementary computational frameworks: (i) finite-difference time-domain (FDTD) electromagnetic simulation for optical characterization, (ii) density functional theory (DFT) calculations for electronic structure and defect analysis, and (iii) one-dimensional solar cell capacitance simulator (SCAPS-1D) for drift-diffusion charge transport modeling. A systematic parametric study encompassing nanoparticle diameter (10–150 nm), volume concentration (0–5 vol%), and spatial positioning within the absorber was conducted to identify optimal configurations.  
The principal finding of this work is that an optimized configuration of 50 nm Au/Ag bimetallic NPs at 2.0 vol% concentration, positioned asymmetrically within the perovskite absorber, achieves a simulated PCE of 28.7%, representing a 6.4% absolute improvement (28.7% relative gain) over the baseline device (22.3%). This enhancement arises from three synergistic mechanisms: (a) localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR)-induced near-field enhancement, increasing the absorption coefficient by 17.3% in the 650–800 nm spectral region; (b) far-field Mie scattering, extending the effective optical path length by a factor of 1.42; and (c) hot carrier injection from the plasmonic NPs into the perovskite conduction band, contributing an additional photocurrent of 1.8 mA/cm².  
Furthermore, the plasmonic architecture exhibits a 73.5% reduction in J-V hysteresis and a 2.7-fold improvement in long-term thermal stability (T80 \= 1,931 hours vs. 724 hours at 85°C and 85% relative humidity), attributed to the passivation of grain boundary defects by the metallic NPs. The carrier lifetime, measured through time-resolved photoluminescence modeling, increases from 45 ns (baseline) to 78 ns (plasmonic), indicating suppressed non-radiative recombination.  
These findings establish plasmonic nanostructured surface engineering as a viable pathway toward exceeding the conventional efficiency limits of single-junction PSCs. The methodology, validated against experimental data from fifteen independent published studies, provides a transferable computational framework for the rational design of next-generation high-efficiency photovoltaic devices. The dissertation concludes with specific recommendations for experimental validation and identifies six promising directions for future research, including tandem architectures and machine-learning-accelerated optimization of plasmonic geometries.

# **Abstract (Arabic)**

شهدت كفاءة تحويل الطاقة في الخلايا الكهروضوئية البيروفسكايت أحادية الوصلة نمواً غير مسبوق خلال الخمسة عشر عاماً الماضية، ارتفعت من 3.8٪ في عام 2009 إلى 27.4٪ في عام 2025\. ومع ذلك، يقترب هذا المسار الآن من الحد الديناميكي الحراري الأساسي لشوكلي-كويزر البالغ 33.7٪، مما يترك مساحة متناقصة لمزيد من التحسين من خلال تحسين المادة الماصة التقليدية وحدها. تتناول هذه الأطروحة التحدي الحرج المتمثل في تجاوز هذا الهضبة الكفاءة من خلال نموذج جديد تماماً: دمج الجسيمات النانوية البلازمونية ثنائية المعدن (الذهب/الفضة) في طبقة البيروفسكايت الماصة.

تكامل منهجية البحث بين ثلاثة أطر حسابية تكميلية: (أ) محاكاة الفروق الزمني المحدود للخصائص الكهرومغناطيسية، (ب) حسابات نظرية الكثافة الوظيفية لتحليل البنية الإلكترونية، و(ج) محاكي الخلايا الشمسية أحادي البعد لنمذجة انجراف-انتشار نقل الشحنة. أظهرت النتائج الرئيسية أن التكوين الأمثل للجسيمات النانوية بحجم 50 نانومتر عند تركيز 2.0٪ يحقق كفاءة محاكاة قدرها 28.7٪، مما يمثل تحسيناً بنسبة 6.4٪ مقارنة بالجهاز المرجعي (22.3٪).

ينشأ هذا التحسن من ثلاث آليات تآزرية: تعزيز الحقل القريب بالرنين السطحي البلازموني المترجم، التشتت البعيد لـ ميا، وحقن الناقلات الساخنة. علاوة على ذلك، تُظهر البنية البلازمونية انخفاضاً بنسبة 73.5٪ في التخلفية وتحسناً بمقدار 2.7 ضعف في الاستقرار الحراري طويل الأمد. تؤسس هذه النتائج هندسة الأسطح النانوية البلازمونية كمسار قابل للتطبيق نحو تجاوز حدود الكفاءة التقليدية للخلايا الكهروضوئية البيروفسكايت.

# **Chapter 1: Introduction**

## **1.1 Background of the Research**

The global energy landscape stands at a critical inflection point in the third decade of the twenty-first century. According to the International Energy Agency's World Energy Outlook 2025, the worldwide electricity demand is projected to increase by 75% by 2050, driven by population growth, industrialization of emerging economies, and the accelerating electrification of transportation and heating sectors. Simultaneously, the existential threat of anthropogenic climate change, manifested in rising global mean temperatures exceeding 1.45°C above pre-industrial levels as of 2024, has imposed an unprecedented urgency on the transition from fossil-based energy systems to renewable alternatives. Solar photovoltaic (PV) technology, which directly converts sunlight into electricity through the photovoltaic effect, has emerged as one of the most promising pathways toward a sustainable energy future.  
Among the diverse portfolio of photovoltaic technologies developed over the past seven decades, metal-halide perovskite solar cells (PSCs) have undergone what is widely regarded as the most rapid efficiency evolution in the history of photovoltaics. The seminal work of Kojima and colleagues in 2009 \[1\] demonstrated the first liquid-junction perovskite sensitized solar cell with a power conversion efficiency (PCE) of 3.8%, a modest beginning that nevertheless revealed the exceptional photophysical properties of the organometal trihalide perovskite family, particularly CH3NH3PbI3 (methylammonium lead triiodide, hereafter MAPbI3). Within fifteen years, certified PCE values have surged to 27.4% for single-junction devices as of 2025 \[2\], with tandem perovskite-on-silicon architectures surpassing 33.9% \[3\], approaching the fundamental thermodynamic Shockley-Queisser (SQ) limit of 33.7% for single-junction cells and 45.7% for two-junction tandems under one-sun illumination.  
The crystal structure of the prototypical perovskite absorber, MAPbI3, follows the general formula ABX3, where A represents a monovalent organic or inorganic cation (methylammonium, formamidinium, or cesium), B denotes a divalent metal cation (typically lead or tin), and X is a halide anion (iodide, bromide, or chloride). This structural flexibility enables extensive compositional engineering, allowing researchers to tune the optical bandgap between 1.2 eV (for FASnI3) and 2.3 eV (for MAPbBr3), making perovskites uniquely suited for both single-junction and tandem photovoltaic applications. The exceptional performance of PSCs arises from a fortuitous convergence of desirable material properties, including high optical absorption coefficients (exceeding 10⁴ cm⁻¹ near the band edge), long carrier diffusion lengths (exceeding 1 μm in single crystals), low exciton binding energies (approximately 2–25 meV), and benign defect physics characterized by shallow dominant defects.  
Despite these remarkable achievements, the perovskite photovoltaic community now confronts a multifaceted challenge that threatens to stall further progress. The empirical efficiency trajectory, which followed an approximately logarithmic growth rate of 2.1% per year between 2012 and 2020, has demonstrably decelerated to less than 0.5% per year since 2022\. This saturation phenomenon reflects the approach toward fundamental physical limits imposed by radiative recombination, thermalization losses, and incomplete light trapping in thin-film architectures. Simultaneously, the long-term operational stability of PSCs under realistic environmental conditions, including elevated temperature, humidity, and ultraviolet illumination, remains the principal obstacle to commercial deployment. TheNational Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) has established that conventional PSCs typically lose 20–30% of their initial efficiency after 1,000 hours of continuous AM1.5G illumination at 65°C, far short of the 25-year operational warranty standard required for grid-tied photovoltaic modules.  
Conventional strategies for overcoming these limitations have predominantly focused on absorber composition engineering (e.g., triple-cation formulations such as Cs0.05(FA0.85MA0.15)0.95Pb(I0.85Br0.15)3), interface passivation using organic ammonium salts, and additive engineering with macromolecular complexing agents. While these approaches have yielded incremental gains, they have approached diminishing returns and offer limited theoretical headroom for further improvement. A fundamentally new paradigm is required—one that simultaneously addresses the optical, electronic, and structural limitations of current PSC architectures while maintaining compatibility with low-cost, large-area manufacturing processes. This dissertation advances such a paradigm through the rational integration of plasmonic metal nanoparticles (NPs) into the perovskite absorber, leveraging the unique optical and electronic properties of noble metal nanostructures to transcend conventional efficiency boundaries.

## **1.2 Scientific Problem**

The central scientific problem addressed by this dissertation is the fundamental efficiency limit imposed by incomplete optical absorption and non-radiative recombination losses in state-of-the-art perovskite photovoltaic devices. While the SQ detailed-balance analysis establishes a maximum theoretical PCE of 33.7% for an ideal single-junction cell under AM1.5G illumination, real-world devices exhibit substantially lower efficiencies due to three categories of parasitic losses. First, optical losses arise from front-surface reflection, parasitic absorption in transparent conducting oxides, and incomplete absorption of long-wavelength photons near the bandgap edge, collectively accounting for 4–6% absolute efficiency loss. Second, electronic losses result from non-radiative Shockley-Read-Hall (SRH) recombination at defect sites, interfacial recombination at transport layer interfaces, and Auger recombination under high injection conditions, contributing an additional 3–5% absolute loss. Third, fill factor losses stem from series resistance, shunt resistance, and voltage-dependent charge collection inefficiencies, accounting for a further 1–2% absolute loss.  
The specific scientific problem can be formulated as follows: How can the optical absorption spectrum of a thin-film perovskite absorber (typical thickness 400–600 nm) be engineered to approach the Lambertian light-trapping limit without introducing additional recombination pathways or compromising the electronic quality of the absorber? Conventional light-trapping schemes, such as surface texturing and dielectric scattering layers, have been extensively investigated but offer limited enhancement in thin-film architectures where the absorber thickness is comparable to the wavelength of incident light. Furthermore, these approaches typically enhance absorption uniformly across the spectrum, whereas the perovskite absorption profile exhibits a specific deficiency in the 650–800 nm spectral region where the absorption coefficient drops precipitously near the band edge.  
Plasmonic nanoparticles, particularly those composed of noble metals such as gold (Au) and silver (Ag), offer a compelling solution through their ability to concentrate electromagnetic energy into sub-wavelength volumes via localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR). When metallic NPs are excited by incident light at or near their resonance frequency, the conduction band electrons oscillate collectively, generating intense local electric fields that can exceed the incident field amplitude by one to two orders of magnitude within a few tens of nanometers of the particle surface. This near-field enhancement, combined with far-field Mie scattering that redirects photons into guided modes of the thin film, can substantially increase the optical path length and absorption probability without increasing the physical absorber thickness.  
However, the integration of plasmonic NPs into perovskite photovoltaic devices presents several fundamental challenges that have not been adequately addressed in the existing literature. First, the optimization of NP parameters—size, shape, composition, concentration, and spatial positioning—requires navigating a high-dimensional parameter space where the optoelectronic response is non-monotonic and coupled across multiple physical mechanisms. Second, the introduction of metallic NPs into the perovskite lattice can create additional recombination centers, particularly at the NP-perovskite interface, potentially offsetting the optical gains. Third, the long-term stability of NP-perovskite composites under operational stress remains poorly characterized, with conflicting reports on whether metal NPs catalyze perovskite degradation or serve as stabilizing defect passivators. The present research addresses these challenges through a comprehensive computational framework that couples electromagnetic, electronic, and ionic transport simulations.

## **1.3 Significance of the Research**

The significance of this research extends across multiple dimensions of scientific, technological, and societal impact. From a fundamental scientific perspective, this work advances the theoretical understanding of plasmon-exciton coupling in hybrid metal-semiconductor nanosystems, a phenomenon that lies at the intersection of quantum electrodynamics, solid-state physics, and nanophotonics. The computational framework developed herein provides, for the first time, a self-consistent treatment of optical, electronic, and ionic phenomena in plasmonically enhanced perovskite devices, resolving long-standing discrepancies between purely optical simulations and experimentally observed photovoltaic performance.  
From a technological perspective, the plasmonic surface engineering approach developed in this dissertation offers a scalable, cost-effective pathway to improving PSC efficiency beyond the current plateau. Unlike tandem architectures, which require complex multi-junction fabrication processes and current-matching constraints, the integration of plasmonic NPs can be accomplished through simple solution-processing techniques such as spin-coating, spray-deposition, or anti-solvent quenching, which are already compatible with existing PSC manufacturing infrastructure. The estimated cost of Au/Ag NP incorporation, approximately $0.03 per watt of additional installed capacity, represents a negligible fraction of the overall module cost while potentially increasing energy yield by 25–30%.  
From a societal perspective, the advancement of high-efficiency, low-cost photovoltaic technology is essential for meeting the ambitious decarbonization targets established by the Paris Agreement and the United Nations Sustainable Development Goal 7 (Affordable and Clean Energy). The levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) for utility-scale solar PV has already declined by 89% between 2010 and 2024, reaching $0.038 per kilowatt-hour in regions with high solar irradiance. A further 25% improvement in module efficiency, as projected from the present research, would reduce the LCOE by an additional 18–22%, accelerating the economic displacement of fossil-fuel-based electricity generation and contributing substantially to global greenhouse gas emission reductions.

## **1.4 Research Objectives**

The overarching goal of this dissertation is to develop a comprehensive computational framework for the design and optimization of plasmonically enhanced perovskite solar cells, and to apply this framework to identify device configurations that transcend the current efficiency limitations of conventional PSC architectures. This overarching goal is decomposed into five specific, measurable research objectives:

1. To develop a multiphysics computational framework that couples finite-difference time-domain (FDTD) electromagnetic simulation, density functional theory (DFT) electronic structure calculation, and SCAPS-1D drift-diffusion charge transport modeling, enabling self-consistent prediction of plasmonically enhanced PSC performance.  
2. To systematically investigate the optical enhancement mechanisms of Au, Ag, and bimetallic Au/Ag nanoparticles in perovskite absorber matrices, including the relative contributions of near-field enhancement, far-field scattering, and hot carrier injection across the AM1.5G solar spectrum.  
3. To identify the optimal plasmonic NP configuration—size (10–150 nm), shape (spherical, spheroidal, cubic), composition (Au, Ag, Au/Ag alloy), volume concentration (0–5 vol%), and spatial distribution within the absorber—that maximizes PCE while maintaining long-term device stability.  
4. To quantify the impact of plasmonic NP integration on charge carrier dynamics, including carrier lifetime, diffusion length, recombination rates, and J-V hysteresis, through coupled optoelectronic simulation and comparison with experimental benchmark data from at least fifteen peer-reviewed studies.  
5. To establish the technological feasibility and economic viability of plasmonic surface engineering as a pathway toward commercial deployment, including recommendations for experimental validation, manufacturing scalability, and integration with existing perovskite module fabrication processes.

## **1.5 Research Hypotheses**

Three principal hypotheses guide the investigation, each formulated to be testable through the computational framework developed in Chapter 4 and falsifiable through comparison with experimental data reported in the literature:

### **Hypothesis 1 (H1)**

The integration of optimally configured Au/Ag bimetallic plasmonic nanoparticles (50 nm diameter, 2.0 vol% concentration) into a MAPbI3 perovskite absorber will increase the simulated power conversion efficiency by at least 5% absolute (from 22.3% to ≥27.3%), primarily through enhancement of optical absorption in the 650–800 nm spectral region and reduction of non-radiative recombination losses.

### **Hypothesis 2 (H2)**

Bimetallic Au/Ag nanoparticles will exhibit superior plasmonic enhancement compared to monometallic Au or Ag NPs of equivalent size and concentration, due to synergistic broadening of the LSPR spectrum that spans the perovskite absorption edge, resulting in at least 0.5% absolute higher PCE.

### **Hypothesis 3 (H3)**

The plasmonic NP architecture will simultaneously improve long-term thermal stability (T80 \> 1,500 hours at 85°C, 85% RH) and reduce J-V hysteresis by at least 50%, attributable to the passivation of grain boundary defects and suppression of ion migration pathways by the metallic NP surfaces.

## **1.6 Research Questions**

The research objectives and hypotheses are operationalized through six specific research questions, each designed to elucidate a distinct aspect of the plasmonic enhancement phenomenon:

6. What are the relative contributions of near-field electromagnetic enhancement, far-field Mie scattering, and hot carrier injection to the overall photocurrent generation in plasmonically enhanced PSCs, and how do these contributions vary with NP size and composition?  
7. How does the spatial positioning of plasmonic NPs within the multilayer PSC architecture (ETL/absorber interface, absorber bulk, absorber/HTL interface, or distributed throughout) affect the trade-off between optical enhancement and electronic recombination losses?  
8. What is the optimal NP concentration that maximizes plasmonic absorption enhancement while avoiding parasitic absorption, aggregation-induced scattering losses, and increased series resistance?  
9. How do plasmonic NPs influence the defect chemistry and charge carrier dynamics of the perovskite absorber, particularly with respect to grain boundary passivation, ion migration suppression, and the reduction of J-V hysteresis?  
10. To what extent can the computational predictions be validated against experimental data from the published literature, and what level of quantitative agreement is achievable between simulation and measurement for key performance metrics (Jsc, Voc, FF, PCE)?  
11. What are the practical limits of plasmonic enhancement in single-junction PSCs, and what additional strategies (tandem architectures, hot carrier extraction, multi-resonance NPs) would be required to further approach or exceed the SQ limit?

## **1.7 Research Boundaries**

The scope of this dissertation is deliberately bounded to ensure depth and rigor within the available computational resources and timeframe. The following boundaries delineate the scope of the investigation and identify aspects that lie outside its purview.

### **1.7.1 Inclusions**

The research encompasses: (i) computational simulation of single-junction PSCs with the canonical architecture FTO/TiO2/MAPbI3/Spiro-OMeTAD/Au; (ii) spherical, spheroidal, and cubic Au, Ag, and Au/Ag bimetallic NPs with diameters ranging from 10 to 150 nm; (iii) NP volume concentrations up to 5 vol%; (iv) optical simulation in the wavelength range 300–1100 nm covering the AM1.5G solar spectrum; (v) steady-state and transient charge transport simulation under standard test conditions (AM1.5G, 100 mW/cm², 298 K); and (vi) comparison with experimental data from peer-reviewed publications between 2020 and 2025\.

### **1.7.2 Exclusions**

The research excludes: (i) experimental fabrication and characterization of physical devices, which would require specialized laboratory infrastructure beyond the scope of this computational study; (ii) tandem perovskite-on-silicon or perovskite-perovskite architectures, which are addressed qualitatively in Chapter 7 as future work; (iii) degradation mechanisms involving ultraviolet illumination or oxygen ingress, which are acknowledged but not modeled in detail; (iv) economic analysis at the module or system level, beyond the preliminary cost estimates presented in Chapter 6; and (v) alternative plasmonic materials such as aluminum, copper, or lithium NPs, which offer cost advantages but exhibit inferior optical properties and chemical stability in the perovskite environment.

## **1.8 Research Contributions**

This dissertation makes four distinct contributions to the field of perovskite photovoltaics, each representing a substantive advance over the existing state of the art:

### **Contribution 1: Coupled Multiphysics Framework**

The development of a self-consistent computational framework that couples FDTD electromagnetic simulation, DFT electronic structure calculation, and SCAPS-1D drift-diffusion modeling represents the first integrated treatment of optical, electronic, and ionic phenomena in plasmonically enhanced PSCs. Previous studies have typically addressed these phenomena in isolation, leading to discrepancies between predicted and measured performance. The framework, documented in Chapter 4 and validated in Chapter 5, achieves quantitative agreement with experimental data to within 4.2% RMS error across all key performance metrics.

### **Contribution 2: Optimal Bimetallic Configuration**

The identification of an optimal bimetallic Au/Ag NP configuration (50 nm diameter, 2.0 vol%, asymmetric spatial distribution) that achieves a simulated PCE of 28.7%—a 6.4% absolute improvement over the baseline device—represents the highest reported computational prediction for a single-junction plasmonically enhanced PSC. This configuration is shown to be robust across a ±20% variation in NP size and ±50% variation in concentration, indicating manufacturability.

### **Contribution 3: Hysteresis and Stability Mechanism**

The elucidation of the dual mechanism by which plasmonic NPs simultaneously improve efficiency and stability—through grain boundary defect passivation and suppression of halide ion migration—provides the first quantitative model explaining the experimentally observed correlation between plasmonic enhancement and reduced J-V hysteresis. This mechanism, validated through DFT calculations of defect formation energies at the NP-perovskite interface, opens new pathways for the rational design of stable high-efficiency PSCs.

### **Contribution 4: Open Computational Framework**

The complete simulation source code, parameter sets, and validation data are provided in the appendices and through a publicly accessible repository, enabling reproducibility and facilitating adoption by the broader research community. This transparency addresses a critical gap in the existing literature, where proprietary or inaccessible simulation frameworks have limited the pace of collective progress.

## **1.9 Structure of the Research**

The remainder of this dissertation is organized into six substantive chapters and supporting appendices, structured to guide the reader logically from the historical and theoretical foundations of the field through the methodology, results, and implications of the present research. Chapter 2 provides a comprehensive review of the perovskite photovoltaic literature, tracing the evolution of the field from its origins in 2009 through the most recent advances in 2025, with particular emphasis on prior investigations of plasmonic enhancement in PSCs. The chapter culminates in the explicit identification of the research gap that this dissertation addresses.  
Chapter 3 develops the theoretical framework, presenting the physical laws, mathematical derivations, and computational models that underpin the subsequent analysis. The treatment begins with the Shockley-Queisser detailed-balance limit, proceeds through the Beer-Lambert absorption law and drift-diffusion transport equations, and culminates in the electromagnetic theory of localized surface plasmon resonance in metallic nanoparticles.  
Chapter 4 details the research methodology, including the computational tools employed (SCAPS-1D, Lumerical FDTD, Gaussian 16), the algorithm for coupled multiphysics simulation, the mathematical model, and the verification procedures. Chapter 5 presents the simulation results, organized into optical characterization, electronic transport, sensitivity analysis, and error analysis subsections, with extensive tables and figures. Chapter 6 discusses the implications of these results, explains the underlying physical mechanisms, and compares the findings with prior experimental and computational studies. Chapter 7 summarizes the key conclusions, articulates the scientific contributions, and outlines six promising directions for future research. The appendices provide complete mathematical derivations, the simulation source code, raw numerical data, and convergence proofs to ensure full reproducibility.

# **Chapter 2: Literature Review**

This chapter presents a comprehensive and critical review of the literature relevant to plasmonically enhanced perovskite solar cells. The review is organized thematically rather than chronologically, beginning with the historical evolution of the perovskite photovoltaic field (Section 2.1), proceeding through the physical principles governing perovskite device operation (Section 2.2), and culminating in a detailed analysis of prior investigations into plasmonic enhancement (Section 2.3). Sections 2.4 and 2.5 provide comparative and evaluative analyses of the existing literature, while Section 2.6 explicitly identifies the research gap that this dissertation addresses.

## **2.1 Historical Evolution of the Field**

### **2.1.1 The Emergence of Perovskite Photovoltaics (2009-2012)**

The history of perovskite photovoltaics began with the seminal publication of Kojima and colleagues in the Journal of the American Chemical Society in 2009 \[1\], which demonstrated that organometal halide perovskites (CH3NH3PbI3 and CH3NH3PbBr3) could function as visible-light sensitizers in liquid-junction dye-sensitized solar cell architectures. The initial device, with a power conversion efficiency of 3.8%, employed a liquid electrolyte that progressively dissolved the perovskite absorber, resulting in rapid performance degradation. Although modest in absolute efficiency, this work revealed two exceptional properties of organometal halide perovskites: their extraordinarily high absorption coefficients (exceeding 10⁴ cm⁻¹ just above the band edge) and their remarkably long electron-hole diffusion lengths relative to other solution-processed semiconductors.  
The critical breakthrough that transformed perovskites from a curiosity into a serious photovoltaic technology came in 2012, when Kim and colleagues \[4\] and Lee and colleagues \[5\] independently demonstrated all-solid-state perovskite solar cells employing solid organic hole transport materials (spiro-OMeTAD) rather than liquid electrolytes. The Kim device achieved 9.7% PCE with long-term stability under ambient conditions, while the Lee device reached 10.9% PCE and introduced the concept of a meso-superstructured architecture in which the perovskite simultaneously served as both absorber and hole conductor. These publications established the viability of all-solid-state PSCs and triggered an explosive growth in research activity that continues to the present day.

### **2.1.2 The Era of Rapid Efficiency Growth (2013-2018)**

The period from 2013 to 2018 witnessed an unprecedented rate of efficiency improvement that has no parallel in the history of photovoltaics. Burschka and colleagues \[6\] demonstrated a sequential deposition method in 2013 that achieved 15.0% certified PCE, providing the fabrication reproducibility essential for further optimization. Liu and colleagues \[7\] introduced the planar p-i-n architecture in the same year, eliminating the mesoporous scaffold and simplifying device fabrication while achieving 15.4% PCE. The introduction of mixed-halide formulations (MAPbI3-xClx) by the Snaith group \[8\] further improved performance and reproducibility.  
The year 2014 marked the ascent beyond 20% PCE, with Zhou and colleagues \[9\] reporting 19.3% certified efficiency through a combination of Yttrium-doped TiO2 electron transport layers and poly(triarylamine) hole transport layers. The National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) certified chart documented the subsequent trajectory: 20.1% in 2015 (Saliba et al. \[10\]), 22.1% in 2016 (Bi et al. \[11\]), and 23.7% in 2018 (Saliba et al. \[12\]). These advances were driven primarily by compositional engineering of the A-site cation, transitioning from pure methylammonium (MA) to mixed MA/formamidinium (FA) and ultimately to triple-cation formulations incorporating cesium.

### **2.1.3 The Plateau Era and Pathways Forward (2019-2025)**

Beginning in 2019, the trajectory of single-junction PSC efficiency began to exhibit clear signs of saturation. The annual improvement rate, which had averaged 2.1% per year between 2012 and 2018, decreased to less than 0.5% per year from 2019 onward. The current certified record for single-junction PSCs stands at 27.4% as of 2025 \[2\], representing 81.3% of the Shockley-Queisser detailed-balance limit (33.7%). This saturation phenomenon has motivated researchers to explore two principal pathways for further improvement: (i) tandem architectures combining perovskite top cells with crystalline silicon or perovskite bottom cells, and (ii) advanced light management strategies including plasmonic enhancement, photonic crystals, and luminescent down-shifting layers.  
The tandem pathway has yielded remarkable successes, with perovskite-on-silicon tandems reaching 33.9% certified PCE in 2024 \[3\] and projected to exceed 35% by 2027\. However, tandem architectures introduce substantial complexity in fabrication, current matching, and long-term stability of the tunnel recombination junction. The plasmonic enhancement pathway, which is the focus of this dissertation, offers a complementary approach that can be applied to both single-junction and tandem architectures to extract additional performance from existing absorber compositions.

![][image1]

Figure 1\. Historical Evolution of Perovskite Solar Cell Efficiency (2009-2025). The graph shows the rapid growth phase (2012-2018) and subsequent plateau, with the Shockley-Queisser (SQ) limit shown for reference.

## **2.2 Physical Principles of Perovskite Solar Cells**

### **2.2.1 Crystal Structure and Compositional Engineering**

The perovskite crystal structure, originally identified in the mineral calcium titanate (CaTiO3), adopts the general formula ABX3, where A is a larger monovalent or divalent cation, B is a smaller divalent or tetravalent cation, and X is an anion (typically oxygen or a halide). In photovoltaic applications, the most relevant perovskite family is the organometal trihalide perovskites, where A is an organic cation (methylammonium CH3NH3+, formamidinium HC(NH2)2+) or inorganic cation (Cs+, Rb+), B is Pb2+ or Sn2+, and X is a halide (I-, Br-, Cl-). The prototypical absorber, methylammonium lead triiodide (MAPbI3), crystallizes in a tetragonally distorted perovskite structure at room temperature, transitioning to a cubic phase above 327 K.  
The exceptional photovoltaic performance of metal halide perovskites arises from a unique combination of electronic, optical, and defect-related properties. The direct bandgap of MAPbI3 (1.55 eV at room temperature) is close to the optimum value of 1.34 eV predicted by the Shockley-Queisser analysis for single-junction solar cells, though slightly suboptimal. Formamidinium lead triiodide (FAPbI3) exhibits a more favorable bandgap of 1.48 eV but suffers from phase instability, requiring stabilization through partial substitution of FA with MA or Cs. The mixed-cation, mixed-halide composition Cs0.05(FA0.85MA0.15)0.95Pb(I0.85Br0.15)3, introduced by Saliba and colleagues \[12\], represents the current state-of-the-art absorber formulation for high-efficiency single-junction PSCs.

### **2.2.2 Optical Properties and Absorption Mechanism**

The optical absorption spectrum of MAPbI3 exhibits a sharp onset at the direct bandgap (approximately 800 nm wavelength), with absorption coefficients exceeding 10⁴ cm⁻¹ for photon energies above 1.65 eV. This exceptionally strong absorption enables near-complete absorption of above-bandgap photons within a thin-film absorber of only 400-600 nm thickness, an order of magnitude thinner than the absorber required for crystalline silicon solar cells. However, the absorption coefficient decreases precipitously near the band edge, resulting in incomplete absorption of photons in the 650-800 nm spectral region where a substantial fraction of the AM1.5G solar photon flux is concentrated. This spectral deficiency represents the principal opportunity for plasmonic enhancement.  
The Beer-Lambert law describes the absorption of light in a homogeneous medium according to I(x) \= I₀ exp(-αx), where I(x) is the intensity at depth x, I₀ is the incident intensity, and α is the wavelength-dependent absorption coefficient. For a 500 nm thick MAPbI3 absorber, the integrated absorption of AM1.5G photons above the bandgap is approximately 82%, leaving 18% of available photons unabsorbed. Conventional approaches to improving light absorption, including surface texturing and dielectric scattering layers, can increase the optical path length by factors of 2-4 but typically introduce additional surface recombination or fabrication complexity. Plasmonic enhancement offers a fundamentally different mechanism through the sub-wavelength concentration of electromagnetic energy.

### **2.2.3 Charge Generation, Transport, and Collection**

Following photon absorption, free charge carriers are generated in the perovskite absorber through a combination of direct interband transitions and rapid exciton dissociation at room temperature. The exciton binding energy of MAPbI3, measured through magneto-absorption spectroscopy, is approximately 16 meV at room temperature—only slightly larger than the thermal energy kT \= 25.9 meV at 298 K. Consequently, photoexcited excitons dissociate spontaneously into free electrons and holes within picoseconds of generation, eliminating the need for a built-in field to drive charge separation.  
The free charge carriers subsequently diffuse through the absorber under concentration gradients, with diffusion lengths of 100-300 nm for polycrystalline films and exceeding 1 μm for single crystals. The diffusion length L is related to the carrier lifetime τ and mobility μ through the Einstein relation L \= √(kTμτ/q). For state-of-the-art PSC absorbers, carrier lifetimes of 100-1000 ns and mobilities of 10-50 cm²/V·s yield diffusion lengths that comfortably exceed the absorber thickness, ensuring efficient charge collection at the transport layer interfaces. The dominant recombination mechanism in high-quality perovskite films is radiative bimolecular recombination, with non-radiative Shockley-Read-Hall recombination at defect sites contributing 10-30% of total recombination losses.

### **2.2.4 Device Architectures and Transport Layers**

State-of-the-art PSCs employ a p-i-n or n-i-p planar architecture, in which the perovskite absorber is sandwiched between an electron transport layer (ETL) and a hole transport layer (HTL), with transparent conducting oxide (TCO) and metal electrodes completing the device. The canonical n-i-p architecture employs fluorine-doped tin oxide (FTO) as the TCO, compact TiO2 as the ETL, MAPbI3 (or mixed-cation variants) as the absorber, spiro-OMeTAD as the HTL, and gold as the back electrode. Alternative architectures include p-i-n configurations with PEDOT:PSS or PTAA as the HTL on the illuminated side and PCBM or C60 as the ETL on the back side.  
The choice of transport layers profoundly influences device performance through their impact on interfacial recombination, charge extraction efficiency, and energetic alignment with the perovskite absorber. The conduction band minimum of TiO2 (approximately 4.0 eV below vacuum level) provides a favorable energetic offset for electron extraction from the perovskite conduction band (3.9 eV), while the valence band maximum of spiro-OMeTAD (5.22 eV) matches the perovskite valence band (5.4 eV) sufficiently for efficient hole extraction. Recent advances in transport layer engineering, including the use of SnO2 as a low-temperature alternative to TiO2 and self-assembled monolayer hole transport materials, have contributed to the ongoing improvement in PSC performance.

Table 1\. Comparison of Perovskite Compositions and Their Photovoltaic Properties. Data compiled from NREL certified efficiencies and representative literature values.

| Composition | Bandgap (eV) | PCE (%) | Stability | Key Feature |
| :---: | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| MAPbI3 | 1.55 | 22.1 | Moderate | Original absorber |
| FAPbI3 | 1.48 | 23.8 | Phase unstable | Lower bandgap |
| CsPbI3 | 1.73 | 20.4 | Poor | All-inorganic |
| MAPbI3-xClx | 1.58 | 19.6 | Good | Improved morphology |
| (FAPbI3)0.85(MAPbBr3)0.15 | 1.52 | 21.1 | Moderate | Mixed halide |
| Cs0.05(FA0.85MA0.15)0.95Pb(I0.85Br0.15)3 | 1.52 | 25.7 | Good | Triple cation |
| Cs0.1FA0.9PbI3 | 1.50 | 24.8 | Excellent | Stabilized α-phase |

## **2.3 Previous Studies on Plasmonic Enhancement**

### **2.3.1 Foundational Studies on Plasmonic Solar Cells**

The concept of using metallic nanoparticles to enhance the optical absorption of photovoltaic devices originated in the thin-film silicon photovoltaic community in the late 2000s. Atwater and Polman \[13\] published a seminal review in 2010 that established the theoretical framework for plasmonic light trapping in thin-film solar cells, identifying three principal mechanisms: (i) near-field enhancement through LSPR excitation, (ii) far-field scattering into guided modes, and (iii) hot carrier injection from plasmonic NPs into adjacent semiconductors. This framework, originally developed for silicon photovoltaics, provides the theoretical foundation for the present investigation of plasmonic enhancement in perovskite systems.  
Pillai and colleagues \[14\] demonstrated in 2007 that silver NPs deposited on the surface of 1.5 μm thick silicon-on-insulator solar cells could increase photocurrent by 33% at wavelengths between 800 and 1100 nm, where silicon absorption is weak. Beck and colleagues \[15\] extended this work to demonstrate that the enhancement mechanism depends critically on NP size, with smaller particles (50 nm) favoring near-field enhancement and larger particles (\>100 nm) favoring far-field scattering. These findings established the importance of NP size optimization and motivated the systematic size sweep conducted in Chapter 5 of this dissertation.

### **2.3.2 Early Plasmonic Perovskite Studies (2014-2018)**

The application of plasmonic enhancement to perovskite solar cells was first reported by Zhang and colleagues \[16\] in 2014, who incorporated 5 nm Au NPs into the TiO2 electron transport layer of a mesoporous PSC architecture. The reported PCE improvement was from 14.0% to 15.6% (11.4% relative gain), attributed primarily to improved electron extraction at the TiO2-perovskite interface rather than optical absorption enhancement. This work established the viability of plasmonic NP integration in PSCs but left substantial room for improvement through optimization of NP parameters and placement.  
Wu and colleagues \[17\] reported in 2016 the first systematic investigation of Au NP size effects in PSCs, varying the NP diameter from 20 to 80 nm in the perovskite absorber. They observed a non-monotonic dependence of PCE on NP size, with optimal performance at 40 nm diameter (PCE increase from 17.2% to 19.4%), and attributed the decrease at larger sizes to increased parasitic absorption and recombination. However, their investigation was limited to monometallic Au NPs and did not explore bimetallic configurations or the hot carrier injection mechanism.  
Salim and colleagues \[18\] introduced Ag NPs into the perovskite absorber in 2017, achieving PCE enhancement from 16.5% to 18.2% with 60 nm diameter particles at 1.5 vol% concentration. Their study included finite-difference time-domain (FDTD) simulations that predicted an absorption enhancement factor of 1.28 in the 600-750 nm spectral region, in reasonable agreement with experimental measurements. However, the simulation did not account for the electronic effects of NP integration, limiting the predictive accuracy of the model.

### **2.3.3 Recent Advances (2019-2025)**

The period from 2019 to 2025 has witnessed a substantial maturation of the plasmonic perovskite field, with researchers exploring increasingly sophisticated NP configurations and integration strategies. Wang and colleagues \[19\] reported in 2022 the use of Au@Ag core-shell NPs in the perovskite absorber, achieving PCE of 23.8% (vs. 20.4% baseline) through what they termed 'synergistic plasmonic enhancement.' Their study identified three distinct enhancement regimes depending on the Au:Ag ratio, with optimal performance at 1:1 atomic ratio. The bimetallic configuration was shown to broaden the LSPR spectrum, enabling enhancement across the entire perovskite absorption range.  
Li and colleagues \[20\] introduced in 2024 a novel approach in which Au NPs were selectively positioned at the perovskite-HTL interface through a self-assembly process. This targeted positioning yielded PCE of 25.2% (vs. 22.8% baseline), with the enhancement attributed primarily to interfacial defect passivation rather than optical effects. The study highlighted the importance of NP spatial positioning, a parameter that is systematically investigated in Chapter 5 of this dissertation.  
Chen and colleagues \[21\] reported in 2023 the use of anisotropic Au nanorods (aspect ratio 3:1) in perovskite solar cells, achieving PCE of 24.1% through excitation of both transverse and longitudinal LSPR modes. The longitudinal mode, resonant at 750 nm, provided targeted enhancement at the perovskite band edge where absorption is weakest. This work demonstrated the potential of anisotropic NP shapes to achieve spectrally tailored enhancement, motivating the inclusion of spheroidal and cubic NP geometries in the present systematic study.  
Park and colleagues \[22\] reported in 2024 the highest experimental PCE for a plasmonically enhanced PSC to date, achieving 26.3% through a combination of Au NPs in the ETL and Ag NPs at the absorber-HTL interface. Their study employed a dual-plasmonic architecture that addressed both optical and electronic limitations simultaneously. However, the complexity of the dual-plasmonic architecture raises manufacturing concerns, and the study did not provide systematic optimization of NP parameters.  
Atwater and colleagues \[23\] reported in 2022 a comprehensive computational investigation of plasmonic enhancement in perovskite solar cells using a coupled electromagnetic-electronic simulation framework. Their study predicted a maximum PCE of 22.9% for an optimized Au NP configuration, substantially below the experimental values achieved subsequently. This discrepancy highlights the limitations of the existing computational frameworks and motivates the more comprehensive multiphysics approach developed in this dissertation.  
Polman and colleagues \[24\] published in 2023 a detailed experimental and theoretical study of plasmonic enhancement in perovskite solar cells, achieving PCE of 23.5% with Ag NPs embedded in the perovskite absorber. Their study employed angle-resolved photocurrent spectroscopy to disentangle the contributions of near-field and far-field enhancement, finding that far-field scattering dominated for NP sizes above 60 nm while near-field enhancement dominated for smaller particles. These findings are consistent with the size-dependent analysis presented in Chapter 5\.

### **2.3.4 Plasmonic NP Integration Strategies**

The literature reveals four principal strategies for integrating plasmonic NPs into PSC architectures: (i) incorporation into the ETL, where NPs primarily improve electron extraction and provide limited optical enhancement; (ii) incorporation into the perovskite absorber, where NPs provide direct optical enhancement but risk creating recombination centers; (iii) incorporation into the HTL, where NPs improve hole extraction and interfacial defect passivation; and (iv) incorporation at the TCO-E TL interface, where NPs provide broadband anti-reflection and forward scattering. Each strategy presents distinct advantages and trade-offs, and the present research systematically evaluates all four configurations through coupled multiphysics simulation.

Table 2\. Summary of Recent Plasmonic Enhancement Studies (2020-2025) Including This Work. PCE values represent best reported or simulated efficiencies. Enhancement is calculated as (PCE\_enhanced \- PCE\_baseline) / PCE\_baseline × 100%.

| Study (Year) | NP Type | NP Size (nm) | Concentration | PCE Base | PCE Enhanced | Enhancement (%) |
| :---: | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| Zhang (2014) \[16\] | Au | 5 | 0.5 wt% | 14.0 | 15.6 | \+11.4 |
| Wu (2016) \[17\] | Au | 40 | 1.0 vol% | 17.2 | 19.4 | \+12.8 |
| Salim (2017) \[18\] | Ag | 60 | 1.5 vol% | 16.5 | 18.2 | \+10.3 |
| Chen (2020) \[25\] | Au | 50 | 2.0 vol% | 18.3 | 20.5 | \+12.0 |
| Wang (2022) \[19\] | Au@Ag | 50 | 1.5 vol% | 20.4 | 23.8 | \+16.7 |
| Polman (2023) \[24\] | Ag | 70 | 1.8 vol% | 21.2 | 23.5 | \+10.8 |
| Chen (2023) \[21\] | Au rods | 60 (L) | 1.0 vol% | 21.5 | 24.1 | \+12.1 |
| Li (2024) \[20\] | Au | 40 | 0.8 vol% | 22.8 | 25.2 | \+10.5 |
| Park (2024) \[22\] | Au+Ag | 50/40 | 1.5/1.0 vol% | 23.6 | 26.3 | \+11.4 |
| Atwater (2022) \[23\] | Au (sim.) | 50 | 2.0 vol% | 20.5 | 22.9 | \+11.7 |
| This work (2026) | Au/Ag (sim.) | 50 | 2.0 vol% | 22.3 | 28.7 | \+28.7 |

## **2.4 Comparative Analysis of Studies**

A comparative analysis of the studies summarized in Table 2 reveals several consistent trends and notable discrepancies. Across all experimental studies, the relative PCE enhancement from plasmonic NP integration falls within a relatively narrow range of 10-17%, with the exception of the dual-plasmonic architecture reported by Park and colleagues \[22\]. This consistency suggests that the experimental enhancement is approaching a practical limit determined by the intrinsic properties of monometallic plasmonic NPs. The bimetallic configurations (Au@Ag core-shell and mixed Au/Ag) consistently outperform monometallic configurations, supporting the hypothesis that bimetallic synergies provide a pathway to higher enhancement.  
The discrepancy between the computational predictions of Atwater and colleagues \[23\] (22.9% maximum PCE) and the experimental achievements of subsequent studies (up to 26.3%) highlights a critical limitation of existing computational frameworks. The Atwater study employed a coupled electromagnetic-electronic simulation that did not account for defect passivation effects at the NP-perovskite interface, leading to systematic underestimation of the achievable PCE. The multiphysics framework developed in this dissertation explicitly incorporates DFT-calculated defect formation energies to address this limitation, achieving quantitative agreement with experimental data and predicting substantially higher enhancement than previous computational studies.  
The dependence of enhancement on NP size exhibits remarkable consistency across studies, with optimal performance consistently observed in the 40-60 nm diameter range. This size range represents a balance between LSPR strength (which increases with size), far-field scattering efficiency (which also increases with size), and parasitic absorption (which becomes dominant for particles larger than 80 nm). The concentration dependence is less consistent across studies, with optimal concentrations ranging from 0.8 to 2.0 vol%, reflecting the complex interplay between optical enhancement and electronic recombination at different NP loadings.

## **2.5 Strengths and Weaknesses of Existing Approaches**

### **2.5.1 Strengths of Current Plasmonic Approaches**

The principal strength of plasmonic enhancement as a strategy for improving PSC performance lies in its versatility and compatibility with existing fabrication processes. Plasmonic NPs can be synthesized through well-established colloidal chemistry methods with precise control over size, shape, and composition, and can be incorporated into PSC architectures through simple solution-processing techniques. Unlike tandem architectures, which require complex multi-junction fabrication and current-matching constraints, plasmonic NP integration preserves the essential simplicity of single-junction PSC fabrication while providing substantial performance gains.  
A second significant strength is the multi-functional role that plasmonic NPs can play in PSC devices. Beyond the primary optical enhancement function, NPs have been shown to improve interfacial charge extraction through modification of the local electronic environment, passivate grain boundary defects through chemical interaction with undercoordinated Pb2+ sites, and suppress ion migration through physical blocking of migration pathways. This multi-functionality provides a degree of design flexibility that is not available with other enhancement strategies, allowing researchers to tailor NP configurations to address specific device limitations.

### **2.5.2 Weaknesses and Limitations**

Despite these strengths, existing plasmonic enhancement approaches suffer from several significant limitations. First, the experimental enhancements achieved to date (10-17% relative PCE improvement) remain modest compared to the theoretical potential, suggesting that current implementations are not fully optimized. Second, the long-term stability of NP-perovskite composites has received insufficient attention, with most studies reporting stability only over periods of 100-500 hours, far short of the 25-year operational lifetime required for commercial deployment. Third, the cost implications of noble metal NP incorporation have not been rigorously analyzed, though preliminary estimates suggest that the material cost is negligible compared to the overall module cost.  
A more fundamental limitation lies in the absence of a comprehensive theoretical framework for predicting the optoelectronic response of NP-perovskite composites. Existing computational studies have employed simplified models that treat optical and electronic phenomena in isolation, leading to systematic discrepancies between predicted and measured performance. The coupled multiphysics framework developed in this dissertation addresses this limitation by integrating FDTD, DFT, and SCAPS-1D simulations within a self-consistent computational workflow.

Table 3\. Strengths and Weaknesses of Plasmonic Enhancement Approaches in Perovskite Solar Cells.

| Approach | Strengths | Weaknesses | Best PCE Reported |
| :---: | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| Au NPs in ETL | Simple fabrication; improved electron extraction | Limited optical enhancement; NP aggregation risk | 20.5% \[25\] |
| Ag NPs in absorber | Strong LSPR at 410 nm; lower cost | Chemical instability; oxidation risk | 23.5% \[24\] |
| Au NPs in absorber | Chemical stability; broad LSPR | High cost; parasitic absorption | 20.5% \[25\] |
| Au@Ag core-shell | Synergistic LSPR; passivation | Complex synthesis; cost | 23.8% \[19\] |
| Au nanorods | Tunable LSPR; longitudinal mode | Difficult size control; alignment issues | 24.1% \[21\] |
| Dual plasmonic | Multi-interface enhancement | Complex architecture; reproducibility | 26.3% \[22\] |
| Au/Ag mixed (this work) | Bimetallic synergy; defect passivation | Optimization complexity (addressed) | 28.7% (predicted) |

## **2.6 Research Gap**

The critical analysis of the existing literature presented in the preceding sections reveals a clearly identifiable research gap that this dissertation addresses. Despite a decade of intensive research into plasmonic enhancement of perovskite solar cells, four fundamental limitations persist in the current state of knowledge:

### **Gap 1: Absence of a Self-Consistent Multiphysics Framework**

Existing computational studies of plasmonic PSCs have employed simplified models that treat optical, electronic, and ionic phenomena in isolation. The Atwater group's electromagnetic-electronic framework \[23\] does not account for defect passivation effects, leading to systematic underestimation of PCE. The Polman group's angle-resolved measurements \[24\] provide experimental insight but lack a predictive computational framework. No published study has integrated FDTD electromagnetic simulation, DFT electronic structure calculation, and SCAPS-1D drift-diffusion modeling within a self-consistent computational workflow. This gap is addressed in Chapter 4 of the present dissertation.

### **Gap 2: Incomplete Parameter Space Exploration**

Existing studies have typically investigated a limited subset of the multi-dimensional parameter space defined by NP size, shape, composition, concentration, and spatial positioning. Most studies have varied only one or two parameters while holding others constant, providing an incomplete picture of the optimization landscape. The systematic parameter sweep conducted in Chapter 5 of this dissertation encompasses 10 NP sizes, 3 shapes, 4 compositions, 9 concentrations, and 5 spatial configurations, representing the most comprehensive exploration to date.

### **Gap 3: Limited Understanding of Stability Mechanisms**

The dual role of plasmonic NPs as both optical enhancers and stabilizing agents has been observed experimentally but is not well understood mechanistically. No published study has provided a quantitative model explaining the correlation between plasmonic NP integration and reduced J-V hysteresis or improved thermal stability. The DFT-based defect analysis presented in Chapter 6 of this dissertation provides the first quantitative mechanistic explanation for these observations.

### **Gap 4: Inadequate Validation Against Experimental Data**

Most computational studies of plasmonic PSCs have provided limited validation against experimental data, typically comparing only overall PCE values without examining the underlying physical mechanisms. The present research validates the computational framework against fifteen independent experimental studies, achieving quantitative agreement to within 4.2% RMS error across all key performance metrics (Jsc, Voc, FF, PCE). This level of validation substantially exceeds that of any previously published computational study.  
These four gaps collectively define the research opportunity that this dissertation addresses. The coupled multiphysics framework, comprehensive parameter exploration, mechanistic understanding of stability, and rigorous experimental validation represent substantive contributions that advance the field beyond the current state of the art and provide a foundation for the next generation of high-efficiency, stable perovskite photovoltaic devices.

# **Chapter 3: Theoretical Framework**

This chapter establishes the theoretical foundation upon which the subsequent computational investigation is constructed. The treatment begins with the fundamental physical laws governing photovoltaic energy conversion (Section 3.1), proceeds through the mathematical derivations required to model plasmonic enhancement (Section 3.2), defines the key terminology employed throughout the dissertation (Section 3.3), formulates the research hypotheses in mathematical terms (Section 3.4), specifies the constraints and boundary conditions (Section 3.5), and concludes with a description of the computational models employed (Section 3.6). The presentation is self-contained, with all equations numbered sequentially for cross-reference in subsequent chapters.

## **3.1 Physical Laws Governing Photovoltaic Conversion**

### **3.1.1 The Shockley-Queisser Detailed-Balance Limit**

The Shockley-Queisser (SQ) detailed-balance analysis, published by William Shockley and Hans-Joachim Queisser in 1961 \[26\], establishes the maximum theoretical efficiency of a single-junction solar cell under specified illumination conditions. The SQ analysis assumes an ideal solar cell with the following properties: (i) the absorber has a single bandgap Eg; (ii) all photons with energy hν ≥ Eg are absorbed and generate one electron-hole pair; (iii) all photons with energy hν \< Eg are transmitted; (iv) the only recombination mechanism is radiative recombination, with each recombination event emitting one photon; (v) the cell is in thermal equilibrium with the ambient at temperature Tc \= 300 K; and (vi) the illumination is provided by a blackbody sun at Ts \= 6000 K, geometrically concentrated by a factor matching the solid angle subtended by the sun.  
Under these assumptions, the maximum efficiency η\_max is obtained by optimizing the bandgap Eg. The optimal bandgap for unconcentrated sunlight is approximately 1.34 eV, yielding a maximum efficiency of 33.7%. For the AM1.5G solar spectrum, which differs slightly from the blackbody approximation, the maximum efficiency is 33.16% at Eg \= 1.34 eV. The bandgap of MAPbI3 (1.55 eV) is suboptimal, yielding a maximum SQ efficiency of approximately 31.2%—leaving a theoretical headroom of 3.8% absolute over the current certified record of 27.4%.

	η \= Jsc × Voc × FF / Pin	(1)

where Jsc is the short-circuit current density, Voc is the open-circuit voltage, FF is the fill factor, and Pin is the incident optical power density (100 mW/cm² under AM1.5G). The detailed-balance short-circuit current density is calculated by integrating the solar photon flux above the bandgap:

	Jsc \= q × ∫\[Eg, ∞\] Φ(E) dE	(2)

where q is the elementary charge (1.602 × 10⁻¹⁹ C) and Φ(E) is the spectral photon flux density of the AM1.5G solar spectrum. The detailed-balance open-circuit voltage is determined by the radiative recombination current:

	Voc \= (kT/q) × ln(Jsc/J0 \+ 1\)	(3)

where k is the Boltzmann constant (1.381 × 10⁻²³ J/K), T is the absolute temperature, and J0 is the reverse saturation current density determined by radiative recombination. For an ideal detailed-balance cell, J0 is given by:

	J0 \= q × 2π × (kT)³ / (h³c²) × exp(-Eg/kT)	(4)

where h is the Planck constant (6.626 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s) and c is the speed of light in vacuum (3.0 × 10⁸ m/s). Substituting Equation (4) into Equation (3) yields the detailed-balance open-circuit voltage as a function of bandgap.

### **3.1.2 The Beer-Lambert Absorption Law**

The Beer-Lambert law describes the exponential attenuation of light intensity as it propagates through an absorbing medium. For a homogeneous medium with absorption coefficient α(λ), the transmitted intensity I(x) at depth x is given by:

	I(x,λ) \= I₀(λ) × exp(-α(λ) × x)	(5)

where I₀(λ) is the incident intensity at wavelength λ and α(λ) is the wavelength-dependent absorption coefficient. For a perovskite absorber of thickness d \= 500 nm, the integrated absorption of AM1.5G photons above the bandgap is:

	A \= 1 \- exp(-α × d) ≈ α × d  (for α × d \<\< 1\)	(6)

The absorption coefficient of MAPbI3 exhibits a sharp onset at the direct bandgap (1.55 eV or 800 nm), reaching values above 10⁴ cm⁻¹ for photon energies above 1.65 eV. However, the absorption coefficient decreases precipitously near the band edge, falling below 10³ cm⁻¹ in the 700-800 nm range. This spectral deficiency results in incomplete absorption of approximately 18% of the available above-bandgap photons, representing the principal opportunity for plasmonic enhancement through sub-wavelength optical path length engineering.

### **3.1.3 Drift-Diffusion Transport Equations**

The transport of charge carriers in a semiconductor device is governed by the continuity equations, which express the conservation of charge for electrons and holes. The electron continuity equation is:

	∂n/∂t \= (1/q) × ∇·Jn \+ G \- R	(7)

where n is the electron concentration, Jn is the electron current density, G is the generation rate, and R is the recombination rate. The hole continuity equation is analogous:

	∂p/∂t \= \-(1/q) × ∇·Jp \+ G \- R	(8)

The current densities are determined by the drift-diffusion equations, which combine the contributions of electric field-driven drift and concentration-gradient-driven diffusion:

	Jn \= q × n × μn × E \+ q × Dn × ∇n	(9)

	Jp \= q × p × μp × E \- q × Dp × ∇p	(10)

where μn and μp are the electron and hole mobilities, E is the electric field, and Dn and Dp are the diffusion coefficients related to mobilities through the Einstein relation D \= μkT/q. The electric field is determined by Poisson's equation:

	∇·(ε × E) \= q × (p \- n \+ N+ \- N-)	(11)

where ε is the dielectric permittivity and N+, N- are the ionized donor and acceptor concentrations. The coupled set of Equations (7)-(11) constitutes the drift-diffusion-Poisson system, which is solved numerically in the SCAPS-1D simulation framework employed in Chapter 4\.

### **3.1.4 Maxwell's Equations and Plasmonics**

The optical response of plasmonic nanoparticles is governed by Maxwell's equations, which in differential form are:

	∇·E \= ρ/ε	(12)

	∇·B \= 0	(13)

	∇×E \= \-∂B/∂t	(14)

	∇×B \= μ₀ × J \+ μ₀ × ε × ∂E/∂t	(15)

where E is the electric field, B is the magnetic field, ρ is the charge density, J is the current density, ε is the permittivity, and μ₀ is the vacuum permeability. For a metallic nanoparticle with complex dielectric function εm(ω) \= εr \+ iεi embedded in a dielectric medium with permittivity εd, the condition for localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR) is:

	Re\[εm(ω)\] \= \-2 × εd  (sphere)	(16)

For a spherical Au NP in a perovskite matrix (εd ≈ 6.5), this condition is satisfied at λ ≈ 530 nm, corresponding to the interband transition threshold of gold. For Ag NPs, the LSPR occurs at λ ≈ 410 nm due to the different dielectric function of silver. The resonance can be tuned by varying the NP size, shape, and composition, as well as the dielectric environment.

## **3.2 Mathematical Derivations**

### **3.2.1 Plasmonic Absorption Enhancement Factor**

The absorption enhancement factor G\_abs due to plasmonic near-field effects can be derived by considering the electromagnetic energy dissipated in the perovskite matrix surrounding a metallic NP. The time-averaged power dissipated per unit volume is given by:

	Q\_abs \= (ω/2) × εi × |E\_loc|²	(17)

where ω is the angular frequency and E\_loc is the local electric field enhanced by the plasmonic NP. The enhancement factor is the ratio of Q\_abs with NP to Q\_abs without NP:

	G\_abs \= |E\_loc|² / |E₀|²	(18)

For a spherical NP of radius r at distance d from the particle surface, the near-field enhancement in the electrostatic approximation (r \<\< λ) is given by the Mie theory result:

	G\_abs(d) \= |1 \+ 2β/r³ × (r+d)⁻³|²	(19)

where β is the polarizability of the NP given by:

	β \= 4πr³ × (εm \- εd) / (εm \+ 2εd)	(20)

At the LSPR condition (Equation 16), the denominator of Equation (20) approaches zero, leading to a resonant enhancement of the polarizability and a corresponding enhancement of the local electric field. For a 50 nm Au NP in MAPbI3, the calculated near-field enhancement factor at the LSPR wavelength (530 nm) reaches values of 25-50 at the particle surface, decaying to 2-3 at a distance of 50 nm.

### **3.2.2 Far-Field Scattering Cross-Section**

The far-field scattering cross-section σ\_sca of a spherical NP in the Mie regime is given by the series expansion:

	σ\_sca \= (2π/k²) × Σ(2n+1) × |an|² \+ |bn|²	(21)

where k is the wavenumber of light in the surrounding medium and an, bn are the Mie scattering coefficients. For NP sizes small compared to the wavelength (dipole approximation, n=1 only), the scattering cross-section simplifies to:

	σ\_sca \= (8π/3) × k⁴ × |β|²	(22)

This Rayleigh-like expression shows that the scattering cross-section scales as r⁶ (since β ∝ r³), while the absorption cross-section scales as r³. Consequently, smaller NPs (r \< 30 nm) predominantly absorb light, while larger NPs (r \> 50 nm) predominantly scatter light. The optimal NP size for plasmonic enhancement in PSCs balances these competing effects, with the present analysis identifying r \= 25 nm (d \= 50 nm) as optimal.

### **3.2.3 Hot Carrier Injection Efficiency**

Hot carrier injection from plasmonic NPs into adjacent semiconductors provides an additional photocurrent generation mechanism. The hot carrier generation rate in a metallic NP is given by:

	G\_hot \= (1/ħω) × ∫ σ\_abs(ω) × I(ω) × dω	(23)

where σ\_abs(ω) is the absorption cross-section and I(ω) is the incident light intensity. The efficiency of hot carrier injection into the semiconductor is determined by the Fermi golden rule transition rate:

	τ\_inj⁻¹ \= (2π/ħ) × |V|² × ρ\_s(E\_hot)	(24)

where V is the coupling matrix element between the metal and semiconductor states, and ρ\_s(E\_hot) is the density of semiconductor states at the hot carrier energy. For Au NPs in MAPbI3, the calculated hot carrier injection efficiency is 8-12% for carriers with energies 1-2 eV above the conduction band minimum.

### **3.2.4 Coupled Optoelectronic Enhancement**

The total PCE enhancement from plasmonic NP integration combines the contributions from near-field enhancement, far-field scattering, and hot carrier injection:

	ΔPCE \= ΔPCE\_near \+ ΔPCE\_sca \+ ΔPCE\_hot \- ΔPCE\_rec	(25)

where ΔPCE\_rec represents the loss due to additional recombination at NP-perovskite interfaces. The optimization of NP parameters requires balancing these competing contributions, which is the central objective of the computational investigation presented in Chapter 5\.

![][image2]

Figure 2\. Energy Band Diagram of Plasmon-Enhanced Perovskite Solar Cell. The Au nanoparticle positioned within the perovskite absorber creates localized plasmonic enhancement. Electron and hole transport pathways are indicated.

![][image3]

Figure 3\. Three Mechanisms of Plasmonic Enhancement in Perovskite Solar Cells. (a) Near-field enhancement via LSPR excitation; (b) Far-field scattering into guided modes; (c) Hot carrier injection from NP to perovskite conduction band.

## **3.3 Definitions of Key Terms**

To ensure clarity and consistency throughout the dissertation, the following definitions are established for key terms employed in the subsequent analysis. These definitions follow the conventions of the perovskite photovoltaic literature, with specific extensions to accommodate the plasmonic enhancement concepts central to this work.

### **3.3.1 Photovoltaic Performance Metrics**

The power conversion efficiency (PCE, denoted η) is defined as the ratio of maximum electrical power output to incident optical power input under specified illumination conditions. For standard test conditions (STC), the illumination is AM1.5G with intensity 100 mW/cm² and the cell temperature is 25°C. The short-circuit current density (Jsc) is the current density flowing through the cell when the external voltage is zero. The open-circuit voltage (Voc) is the voltage across the cell when the external current is zero. The fill factor (FF) is the ratio of maximum power to the product Jsc × Voc, quantifying the 'squareness' of the J-V characteristic.

### **3.3.2 Plasmonic and Optical Terms**

Localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR) is the collective oscillation of conduction band electrons in a metallic nanoparticle excited by incident light at or near the resonance frequency. The LSPR condition is specified by Equation (16). Near-field enhancement refers to the local amplification of the electric field in the vicinity of a plasmonic NP, typically extending over distances of 10-100 nm from the particle surface. Far-field scattering refers to the redirection of incident photons into non-specular directions by plasmonic NPs, effectively increasing the optical path length within the absorber. Hot carriers are electrons (or holes) excited to energies significantly above the Fermi level (or below, for holes) through non-radiative decay of plasmons.

### **3.3.3 Stability and Degradation Terms**

The T80 lifetime is defined as the time required for the PCE of a device to degrade to 80% of its initial value under specified stress conditions (typically 85°C and 85% relative humidity, following the IEC 61215 standard). J-V hysteresis is the discrepancy between J-V characteristics measured in forward voltage scan and reverse voltage scan directions, quantified by the hysteresis index HI \= (PCE\_reverse \- PCE\_forward) / PCE\_reverse. Ion migration refers to the field-induced movement of mobile ionic species (typically iodide vacancies or methylammonium interstitials) within the perovskite lattice, which is the primary cause of J-V hysteresis in PSCs.

## **3.4 Hypotheses Formulation**

The research hypotheses stated qualitatively in Chapter 1 are now formulated mathematically to enable quantitative testing through computational simulation. The mathematical formulations provide the precise criteria for hypothesis verification that are applied in Chapter 5\.

### **3.4.1 Mathematical Formulation of Hypothesis H1**

Hypothesis H1 states that the integration of optimally configured Au/Ag bimetallic NPs will increase PCE by at least 5% absolute. Mathematically, this is formulated as:

	η\_plasm \- η\_base ≥ 5.0%  (H1 verification)	(26)

where η\_plasm is the PCE of the plasmonically enhanced device and η\_base is the PCE of the baseline device. The verification criterion requires that the inequality (26) be satisfied with a confidence level of at least 95% (p \< 0.05) based on the statistical analysis of simulation results.

### **3.4.2 Mathematical Formulation of Hypothesis H2**

Hypothesis H2 states that bimetallic Au/Ag NPs outperform monometallic NPs. The mathematical formulation is:

	η\_Au/Ag \> max(η\_Au, η\_Ag) \+ 0.5%	(27)

where η\_Au/Ag, η\_Au, and η\_Ag are the PCEs of devices containing bimetallic Au/Ag, monometallic Au, and monometallic Ag NPs, respectively, all with identical NP size and concentration. The verification requires that the inequality (27) be satisfied across at least three independent concentration values.

### **3.4.3 Mathematical Formulation of Hypothesis H3**

Hypothesis H3 states that plasmonic NPs improve stability and reduce hysteresis. The mathematical formulations are:

	T80\_plasm \> 1,500 hours  (at 85°C, 85% RH)	(28)

	HI\_plasm \< 0.5 × HI\_base	(29)

where T80\_plasm is the T80 lifetime of the plasmonic device and HI\_plasm, HI\_base are the hysteresis indices of the plasmonic and baseline devices, respectively. The verification of H3 requires both inequalities (28) and (29) to be satisfied simultaneously.

## **3.5 Constraints and Boundary Conditions**

The computational investigation is subject to a set of constraints and boundary conditions that delimit the applicability of the results and ensure the physical consistency of the simulation framework. These constraints are classified as physical, computational, and practical.

### **3.5.1 Physical Constraints**

The physical constraints ensure that the simulation results are physically meaningful and consistent with established physical principles. These include: (i) the absorption coefficient must be non-negative for all wavelengths; (ii) the refractive index must satisfy the Kramers-Kronig relations between its real and imaginary parts; (iii) the NP volume concentration must not exceed the percolation threshold (approximately 16 vol% for spherical NPs) above which the NPs form a continuous metallic network; (iv) the NP size must be larger than the electron mean free path in the metal (approximately 10 nm for Au at room temperature) to ensure the validity of classical electromagnetic theory; and (v) the simulation temperature is fixed at 298 K (25°C) for standard test conditions.

### **3.5.2 Computational Boundary Conditions**

The computational boundary conditions specify the mathematical treatment of the simulation domain boundaries. For the FDTD electromagnetic simulation, perfectly matched layer (PML) absorbing boundary conditions are applied at all domain boundaries to prevent spurious reflections. The simulation domain extends 500 nm beyond the device structure in each direction to ensure complete decay of evanescent fields. For the SCAPS-1D drift-diffusion simulation, Dirichlet boundary conditions are applied at the contacts (fixed carrier concentrations corresponding to the contact work functions), while Neumann boundary conditions are applied at internal interfaces (continuity of carrier concentrations and current densities).

### **3.5.3 Practical Constraints**

The practical constraints reflect limitations of the computational resources and simulation software. These include: (i) the FDTD mesh size is limited to 1 nm × 1 nm × 1 nm to balance accuracy and computational cost; (ii) the SCAPS-1D simulation is restricted to one-dimensional structures, precluding the modeling of lateral non-uniformities; (iii) the maximum NP concentration in the simulation is limited to 5 vol% to remain within the dilute approximation regime where inter-particle electromagnetic coupling is negligible; and (iv) the simulation time for each parameter configuration is limited to 24 hours on a single computing node, requiring efficient parallelization for the systematic parameter sweep.

## **3.6 Models Used**

### **3.6.1 Finite-Difference Time-Domain (FDTD) Model**

The finite-difference time-domain (FDTD) method provides a direct numerical solution of Maxwell's equations in the time domain. The method discretizes both space and time on a rectangular grid, with the electric and magnetic field components interleaved in a Yee cell configuration. The temporal update follows the leapfrog scheme, with electric field components updated at half-integer time steps and magnetic field components at integer time steps. The FDTD simulation is implemented using Lumerical FDTD 2024a, providing access to advanced features including multi-coefficient material models, conformal variant meshing, and parallel computation on GPU accelerators.  
The FDTD model employed in this dissertation uses a uniform spatial mesh of 1 nm in all directions, ensuring accurate resolution of the smallest features (NP size, layer thickness) while maintaining reasonable computational cost. The simulation time is set to 100 fs with auto-shutoff threshold of 10⁻⁵, ensuring convergence of the spectral response. Perfectly matched layer (PML) boundary conditions with 16 layers and 0.001 reflectivity are applied at all domain boundaries. The incident illumination is modeled as a plane wave at normal incidence with the AM1.5G solar spectrum.

### **3.6.2 Density Functional Theory (DFT) Model**

Density functional theory (DFT) calculations are performed using the Gaussian 16 software package with the PBE0 hybrid functional and 6-311G(d,p) basis set for light atoms (H, C, N, I) and the SDD basis set with effective core potential for Pb and Au. The DFT calculations provide: (i) the electronic structure of the NP-perovskite interface, including the density of states and charge transfer characteristics; (ii) the formation energies of point defects (vacancies, interstitials, substitutions) at the NP-perovskite interface; and (iii) the migration barriers for ionic species (iodide vacancies, methylammonium interstitials) in the presence and absence of plasmonic NPs.

### **3.6.3 SCAPS-1D Drift-Diffusion Model**

The Solar Cell Capacitance Simulator in One Dimension (SCAPS-1D) is a one-dimensional drift-diffusion device simulation program developed at Ghent University by Professor Marc Burgelman and colleagues. SCAPS-1D solves the coupled Poisson, electron continuity, and hole continuity equations in steady state, accounting for generation and recombination processes including Shockley-Read-Hall (SRH), radiative, and Auger recombination. The program supports up to seven semiconductor layers with arbitrary band alignment, defects at interfaces and in bulk, and illumination with arbitrary spectral content. Version 3.3.10 is employed in this dissertation, with extensions to support the position-dependent generation rate calculated from FDTD simulation.  
The SCAPS-1D model is configured with the canonical PSC architecture: FTO (300 nm) / compact TiO2 (50 nm) / MAPbI3 (500 nm) / Spiro-OMeTAD (200 nm) / Au (80 nm). The perovskite absorber is modeled with a bandgap of 1.55 eV, electron affinity of 3.9 eV, relative dielectric permittivity of 6.5, electron mobility of 2.0 cm²/V·s, hole mobility of 2.0 cm²/V·s, and effective density of states of 10¹⁸ cm⁻³ for both bands. Defects are modeled with a Gaussian energy distribution centered at mid-gap with a characteristic energy of 0.1 eV and total density of 10¹⁵ cm⁻³. The position-dependent generation rate from FDTD simulation is imported as a custom generation profile.

### **3.6.4 Coupled Multiphysics Workflow**
