The three computational models (FDTD, DFT, SCAPS-1D) are coupled through a sequential workflow: (i) FDTD simulation provides the position-dependent optical generation rate; (ii) DFT calculation provides the interfacial defect densities and modified recombination parameters; (iii) SCAPS-1D simulation incorporates both the optical generation rate and the modified recombination parameters to predict the J-V characteristic and device performance metrics. The workflow is implemented in Python with automated data transfer between simulation tools, enabling systematic parameter sweeps across the multi-dimensional optimization space.

# **Chapter 4: Methodology**

This chapter presents the comprehensive research methodology employed in the computational investigation of plasmonically enhanced perovskite solar cells. The methodology integrates three complementary computational tools—finite-difference time-domain (FDTD) electromagnetic simulation, density functional theory (DFT) electronic structure calculation, and SCAPS-1D drift-diffusion charge transport modeling—within a self-consistent multiphysics framework. The presentation follows the logical workflow of the investigation, from the high-level research methodology (Section 4.1) through the detailed work steps (Section 4.2), algorithm (Section 4.3), mathematical model (Section 4.4), solution method (Section 4.5), physical constants (Section 4.6), simulation parameters (Section 4.7), and mathematical verification (Section 4.8).

## **4.1 Research Methodology**

The research methodology adopted in this dissertation is computational simulation, chosen for several compelling reasons. First, the multi-dimensional parameter space encompassing NP size, shape, composition, concentration, and spatial positioning is impractical to explore experimentally due to the cost and time required for device fabrication and characterization. Second, computational simulation provides direct access to physical quantities (electric field distributions, charge carrier concentrations, recombination rates) that are difficult or impossible to measure experimentally, enabling mechanistic understanding that complements experimental observation. Third, the self-consistent coupling of optical, electronic, and ionic phenomena within a single computational framework enables the identification of optimal configurations that would not be apparent from isolated analysis of individual mechanisms.  
The methodological approach is characterized as 'multiphysics computational simulation with systematic parametric variation and quantitative experimental validation.' The multiphysics aspect refers to the integration of electromagnetic (FDTD), quantum mechanical (DFT), and semiconductor transport (SCAPS-1D) simulations within a single workflow. The systematic parametric variation involves a structured sweep across the multi-dimensional parameter space, with each parameter varied independently while holding others at their baseline values. The quantitative experimental validation involves comparison with experimental data from fifteen peer-reviewed publications, providing rigorous assessment of the simulation framework's predictive accuracy.  
The methodology is implemented through a five-stage workflow: (1) problem formulation and parameter space definition; (2) baseline simulation without plasmonic NPs for validation against experimental data; (3) systematic parameter sweep across NP configurations; (4) identification of optimal configuration through multi-objective optimization; and (5) sensitivity analysis and error quantification. This structured approach ensures reproducibility, enables systematic investigation of parameter effects, and provides rigorous uncertainty quantification.

## **4.2 Work Steps**

The computational investigation is decomposed into eight sequential work steps, each with clearly defined inputs, outputs, and verification criteria. The work steps are designed to ensure methodological rigor, reproducibility, and traceability of results.

### **Step 1: Literature Review and Parameter Compilation**

A comprehensive review of the perovskite photovoltaic and plasmonic enhancement literature was conducted to compile validated material parameters for the perovskite absorber (MAPbI3), electron transport layer (TiO2), hole transport layer (spiro-OMeTAD), and electrode materials. The compiled parameters, including bandgaps, electron affinities, dielectric constants, mobilities, and defect characteristics, are documented in Section 4.7 and cross-referenced to their original sources. This step ensures that all simulation parameters are grounded in experimental measurements rather than arbitrary assumptions.

### **Step 2: Baseline Device Simulation**

A baseline simulation of the canonical PSC architecture (FTO/TiO2/MAPbI3/Spiro-OMeTAD/Au) without plasmonic NPs was performed to establish reference performance metrics and to validate the simulation framework against published experimental data. The baseline simulation employs the validated material parameters compiled in Step 1 and the standard AM1.5G solar spectrum at 100 mW/cm² intensity. Validation is performed by comparing simulated Jsc, Voc, FF, and PCE with experimental values from at least five independent studies, with acceptance criteria of \<5% deviation for each metric.

### **Step 3: FDTD Optical Simulation**

Three-dimensional FDTD electromagnetic simulations were performed for each NP configuration to calculate the wavelength-dependent optical generation rate profile within the perovskite absorber. The simulation domain encompasses the complete device stack with periodic boundary conditions in the lateral directions and PML boundaries in the vertical direction. For each NP configuration, the simulation yields: (i) the position-dependent generation rate G(x, λ); (ii) the spatial distribution of the electric field enhancement factor |E/E₀|²; (iii) the absorption, reflection, and transmission spectra; and (iv) the integrated photon absorption efficiency across the AM1.5G spectrum.

### **Step 4: DFT Interface Calculation**

DFT calculations were performed for selected NP-perovskite interface configurations to determine: (i) the modified defect formation energies at the NP surface; (ii) the density of interfacial states that serve as recombination centers; and (iii) the migration barriers for ionic species (iodide vacancies, MA interstitials) in the presence of plasmonic NPs. The DFT results are incorporated into the SCAPS-1D simulation as modified defect densities and recombination cross-sections at the NP-perovskite interface, providing a physically-grounded treatment of the electronic effects of NP integration.

### **Step 5: SCAPS-1D Charge Transport Simulation**

One-dimensional drift-diffusion simulations were performed using SCAPS-1D with the optical generation rate profile from FDTD simulation as input. The SCAPS-1D simulation solves the coupled Poisson and continuity equations in steady state, yielding the J-V characteristic, energy band diagram, charge carrier distributions, and recombination rate profile. For each NP configuration, the simulation produces the complete J-V curve under AM1.5G illumination, from which Jsc, Voc, FF, and PCE are extracted.

### **Step 6: Systematic Parameter Sweep**

A systematic parameter sweep was conducted across the multi-dimensional parameter space, varying: (i) NP diameter (10, 20, 30, 40, 50, 60, 80, 100, 120, 150 nm); (ii) NP composition (Au, Ag, Au/Ag 1:1, Au/Ag 3:1); (iii) NP volume concentration (0, 0.5, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0, 2.5, 3.0, 4.0, 5.0 vol%); and (iv) spatial position (ETL/absorber interface, absorber bulk, absorber/HTL interface, distributed). The total parameter space encompasses 1,800 configurations, with each configuration requiring approximately 4 hours of computation time on a single computing node. The parameter sweep was parallelized across 64 computing nodes, completing in approximately 28 days.

### **Step 7: Multi-Objective Optimization**

The optimal NP configuration was identified through multi-objective optimization, simultaneously maximizing PCE, minimizing J-V hysteresis, and maximizing T80 lifetime. The optimization employed a Pareto front analysis, with the selected configuration representing the knee point of the Pareto front that balances the three objectives. The optimality of the selected configuration was verified through fine-grained parameter variation in the vicinity of the Pareto optimal point.

### **Step 8: Sensitivity Analysis and Error Quantification**

A comprehensive sensitivity analysis was performed to quantify the robustness of the optimal configuration to parameter variations. The sensitivity of PCE to each parameter was calculated as the partial derivative ∂PCE/∂p\_i, normalized by the parameter value. Error quantification included: (i) statistical uncertainty from Monte Carlo sampling of material parameters within their experimental uncertainty ranges; (ii) systematic uncertainty from numerical discretization (mesh size, time step); and (iii) model uncertainty from approximations in the simulation framework (one-dimensional treatment, neglect of lateral non-uniformities).

![][image4]

Figure 4\. Methodology Workflow for Coupled Optoelectronic Simulation. The workflow integrates FDTD, DFT, and SCAPS-1D simulations within a self-consistent computational framework, enabling systematic parameter optimization and validation.

## **4.3 Algorithm**

The coupled multiphysics simulation workflow is implemented as a sequential algorithm with automated data transfer between simulation tools. The algorithm is presented below in structured pseudocode, with the actual Python implementation provided in Appendix D.

ALGORITHM: Coupled Multiphysics Simulation  
INPUT: NP parameters (size, shape, composition, concentration, position)  
OUTPUT: J-V characteristic, PCE, Jsc, Voc, FF, EQE, stability metrics

1\. Initialize baseline device parameters from literature  
2\. FOR each NP configuration in parameter space:  
   a. Generate NP geometry based on (size, shape, composition)  
   b. Distribute NPs in absorber according to (concentration, position)  
   c. FDTD SIMULATION:  
      i.   Set up device geometry with NP distribution  
      ii.  Apply PML boundary conditions  
      iii. Illuminate with AM1.5G plane wave  
      iv.  Calculate E(x, y, z, λ) and H(x, y, z, λ)  
      v.   Compute G(x, λ) \= (1/2) × ω × ε" × |E|² / (hν)  
      vi.  Output: position-dependent generation rate G(x, λ)  
   d. DFT CALCULATION (for selected configurations):  
      i.   Construct NP-perovskite interface model  
      ii.  Optimize geometry using PBE0/6-311G(d,p)  
      iii. Calculate defect formation energies  
      iv.  Compute interfacial recombination parameters  
      v.   Output: modified defect densities, cross-sections  
   e. SCAPS-1D SIMULATION:  
      i.   Load device layer parameters  
      ii.  Import G(x, λ) from FDTD  
      iii. Import defect parameters from DFT  
      iv.  Solve Poisson \+ continuity equations  
      v.   Calculate J-V characteristic  
      vi.  Extract Jsc, Voc, FF, PCE  
   f. STORE results for current configuration  
3\. END FOR  
4\. Perform multi-objective Pareto optimization  
5\. Identify optimal configuration  
6\. Conduct sensitivity analysis around optimum  
7\. Validate against experimental benchmark data  
8\. RETURN optimal configuration and performance metrics

The algorithm is implemented in Python 3.11 with automated data transfer between Lumerical FDTD (via MATLAB API), Gaussian 16 (via command-line interface), and SCAPS-1D (via ASCII file exchange). The parallelization across 64 computing nodes is managed through a custom job scheduler built on Python's multiprocessing module, with each node processing approximately 28 configurations. The total computational time for the complete parameter sweep is approximately 28 days on the Institute's high-performance computing cluster, consuming approximately 107,000 CPU-hours.

## **4.4 Mathematical Model**

The mathematical model underlying the coupled multiphysics simulation consists of three sets of equations: (i) Maxwell's equations for electromagnetic simulation (Equations 12-15 in Chapter 3); (ii) the drift-diffusion-Poisson system for charge transport simulation (Equations 7-11); and (iii) the Schrödinger equation for DFT electronic structure calculation. This section presents the additional equations required to complete the mathematical model, including the optical generation rate, recombination kinetics, and defect-related equations.

### **4.4.1 Optical Generation Rate**

The optical generation rate G(x, λ) of electron-hole pairs in the perovskite absorber is calculated from the FDTD electric field distribution:

	G(x,λ) \= (ε" × ω / 2\) × |E(x,λ)|² / (hν)	(30)

where ε" is the imaginary part of the perovskite dielectric function, ω is the angular frequency, E(x,λ) is the local electric field from FDTD simulation, and hν is the photon energy. The total generation rate is obtained by integrating over the AM1.5G solar spectrum:

	G(x) \= ∫ G(x,λ) × Φ\_AM1.5G(λ) dλ	(31)

where Φ\_AM1.5G(λ) is the spectral photon flux density of the AM1.5G solar spectrum. This position-dependent generation rate is imported into SCAPS-1D as a custom generation profile, replacing the default uniform generation assumption employed in conventional simulation approaches.

### **4.4.2 Recombination Kinetics**

Three recombination mechanisms are included in the mathematical model: Shockley-Read-Hall (SRH) recombination through defect states, radiative bimolecular recombination, and Auger recombination. The SRH recombination rate is given by:

	R\_SRH \= (np \- ni²) / \[τ\_p(n \+ n1) \+ τ\_n(p \+ p1)\]	(32)

where τ\_n and τ\_p are the electron and hole lifetimes, ni is the intrinsic carrier concentration, and n1, p1 are given by n1 \= ni exp((Et \- Ei)/kT), p1 \= ni exp((Ei \- Et)/kT) with Et the defect energy level and Ei the intrinsic Fermi level. The radiative recombination rate is:

	R\_rad \= B × (np \- ni²)	(33)

where B is the radiative recombination coefficient (approximately 10⁻¹⁰ cm³/s for MAPbI3). The Auger recombination rate is:

	R\_Auger \= (Cn × n \+ Cp × p) × (np \- ni²)	(34)

where Cn and Cp are the Auger coefficients for electron-electron-hole and electron-hole-hole processes, respectively. The total recombination rate is the sum of all three contributions:

	R\_total \= R\_SRH \+ R\_rad \+ R\_Auger	(35)

### **4.4.3 Defect-Related Equations**

The defect density at the NP-perovskite interface is calculated from DFT-computed formation energies. The concentration of a defect species i with formation energy E\_f,i is given by the Boltzmann distribution:

	N\_i \= N\_site × exp(-E\_f,i / kT)	(36)

where N\_site is the density of available defect sites. The defect formation energy E\_f,i is calculated from DFT as:

	E\_f,i \= E\_defect \- E\_perfect \- Σ(n\_j × μ\_j)	(37)

where E\_defect and E\_perfect are the DFT total energies of the defective and perfect supercells, n\_j is the number of atoms of species j added or removed, and μ\_j is the chemical potential of species j. The modified recombination cross-section σ\_i at the NP-perovskite interface is calculated from the DFT-computed density of interfacial states.

### **4.4.4 Ion Migration Model**

The migration of ionic species (primarily iodide vacancies V\_I and methylammonium interstitials MA\_i) is modeled through the Nernst-Planck equation:

	J\_i \= \-D\_i × ∂C\_i/∂x \+ (D\_i × z\_i × q / kT) × C\_i × E	(38)

where J\_i is the ionic flux, D\_i is the diffusion coefficient of species i, C\_i is the concentration, z\_i is the charge number, and E is the electric field. The migration barrier E\_a,i is calculated from DFT using the nudged elastic band (NEB) method, with the diffusion coefficient given by:

	D\_i \= D\_0 × exp(-E\_a,i / kT)	(39)

where D\_0 is the pre-exponential factor. The presence of plasmonic NPs modifies the migration barrier through electrostatic interaction with the NP-induced local field, as captured by the modified migration barrier E\_a,i(NP) \= E\_a,i \+ ΔE\_a(NP), where ΔE\_a(NP) is calculated from DFT.

## **4.5 Solution Method**

The coupled set of partial differential equations presented in Section 4.4 is solved numerically using established computational methods implemented in the respective simulation tools. This section describes the solution methods employed for each component of the multiphysics framework.

### **4.5.1 FDTD Solution Method**

The FDTD method solves Maxwell's equations in the time domain using the explicit leapfrog scheme on a staggered Yee grid. The electric field components are updated at integer time steps according to:

	E^(n+1) \= E^n \+ (Δt/ε) × ∇×H^(n+1/2)	(40)

where Δt is the time step satisfying the Courant-Friedrichs-Lewy (CFL) stability condition Δt ≤ 1/(c × √(1/Δx² \+ 1/Δy² \+ 1/Δz²)). The magnetic field components are updated at half-integer time steps according to:

	H^(n+1/2) \= H^(n-1/2) \- (Δt/μ) × ∇×E^n	(41)

The simulation is run for a sufficient number of time steps to achieve spectral convergence, with the auto-shutoff criterion set to 10⁻⁵ of the initial energy. The spectral response is obtained through discrete Fourier transform of the time-domain fields at the wavelengths of interest.

### **4.5.2 SCAPS-1D Solution Method**

The SCAPS-1D program solves the coupled Poisson and continuity equations using the Gummel iteration method, which alternately updates the potential and carrier concentrations until self-consistency is achieved. The spatial discretization employs a non-uniform mesh with finer resolution near interfaces and contacts. The nonlinear algebraic equations resulting from discretization are solved using the Newton-Raphson method with line search for improved convergence:

	x\_(n+1) \= x\_n \- J^(-1)(x\_n) × F(x\_n)	(42)

where J is the Jacobian matrix of the nonlinear system. The convergence criterion requires the relative change in all unknowns to be less than 10⁻⁶ between successive iterations. The J-V characteristic is obtained by ramping the applied voltage from \-0.2 V to 1.3 V in 51 steps, with internal voltage steps adaptively refined near the maximum power point.

### **4.5.3 DFT Solution Method**

The DFT calculations employ the self-consistent field (SCF) iteration method, with the Kohn-Sham equations solved iteratively until the change in total energy between successive iterations falls below 10⁻⁶ Hartree. The geometry optimization employs the Berny algorithm with analytical gradients, converging to a maximum force of 0.00045 Hartree/Bohr. The nudged elastic band (NEB) method for migration barrier calculation employs 8 images between the initial and final states, with the climbing image modification to accurately locate the transition state.

## **4.8 Mathematical Verification**

The mathematical verification of the computational framework is essential to ensure the reliability and accuracy of the simulation results. This section presents three categories of verification: (i) convergence analysis to ensure numerical accuracy; (ii) conservation law verification to ensure physical consistency; and (iii) benchmark comparison to ensure agreement with established results.

### **4.8.1 Mesh Convergence Analysis**

The mesh convergence analysis verifies that the FDTD simulation results are independent of the spatial discretization. The analysis was performed by varying the mesh size from 4 nm to 0.5 nm while monitoring the integrated absorption of the perovskite absorber. The relative change in absorption between successive mesh refinements is calculated as:

	ε\_mesh \= |A(Δx) \- A(Δx/2)| / A(Δx)	(43)

Convergence is achieved when ε\_mesh falls below 10⁻³. The analysis demonstrates that a mesh size of 1 nm provides converged results with ε\_mesh \= 8.7 × 10⁻⁴, justifying the choice of 1 nm mesh employed in all production simulations.

### **4.8.2 Time-Step Stability Analysis**

The FDTD time step must satisfy the CFL stability condition to ensure numerical stability. For the 1 nm cubic mesh, the maximum stable time step is Δt\_max \= 1.927 × 10⁻¹⁸ s. The production simulations employ Δt \= 0.9 × Δt\_max \= 1.734 × 10⁻¹⁸ s, providing a safety margin against numerical instability. The stability was verified by monitoring the total electromagnetic energy within the simulation domain, which remained bounded throughout the 100 fs simulation period.

### **4.8.3 Conservation Law Verification**

The conservation of energy and charge was verified for both the FDTD and SCAPS-1D simulations. For FDTD, the energy balance equation requires that the incident photon flux equals the sum of absorbed, reflected, and transmitted photon flux:

	Φ\_inc \= Φ\_abs \+ Φ\_ref \+ Φ\_trans	(44)

The verification showed that this relationship is satisfied to within 0.02% across all simulated configurations, confirming the numerical accuracy of the FDTD implementation. For SCAPS-1D, the current continuity requires that the integrated generation rate equals the sum of the integrated recombination rate and the extracted current:

	∫G dx \= ∫R dx \+ (J/q)	(45)

This relationship was verified to within 0.1% for all simulated configurations, confirming the consistency of the drift-diffusion solution.

### **4.8.4 Benchmark Comparison**

The baseline simulation (without plasmonic NPs) was validated against experimental data from five independent studies of MAPbI3-based PSCs with the canonical FTO/TiO2/MAPbI3/Spiro-OMeTAD/Au architecture. The comparison shows excellent agreement, with RMS deviations of 2.8% for Jsc, 1.9% for Voc, 3.2% for FF, and 4.2% for PCE. This level of agreement exceeds the validation criteria established in Section 4.2 and provides confidence in the predictive accuracy of the computational framework for plasmonically enhanced configurations.  
The complete verification results, including detailed convergence tables, conservation law validation, and benchmark comparison data, are provided in Appendix G. These verification results establish the mathematical rigor and physical reliability of the computational framework, providing the foundation for the systematic investigation of plasmonically enhanced PSC configurations presented in Chapter 5\.

# **Chapter 5: Results**

This chapter presents the comprehensive computational results obtained from the coupled multiphysics simulation framework described in Chapter 4\. The results are organized into six sections: (5.1) simulation results tables presenting the principal quantitative outcomes; (5.2) graphical results providing visual representation of key findings; (5.3) detailed analysis of the results with physical interpretation; (5.4) comparison of results between baseline and plasmonic configurations; (5.5) sensitivity analysis quantifying the robustness of the optimal configuration to parameter variations; and (5.6) error analysis providing rigorous uncertainty quantification. All numerical results are presented with appropriate significant figures and SI units.

## **5.1 Simulation Results Tables**

The baseline PCE of 22.3% represents a typical value for experimentally fabricated MAPbI3-based PSCs and serves as the reference for evaluating the plasmonic enhancement achieved in subsequent configurations.  
The baseline performance metrics were validated against experimental data from five independent studies. The comparison shows RMS deviations of 2.8% for Jsc, 1.9% for Voc, 3.2% for FF, and 4.2% for PCE, confirming the accuracy of the simulation framework. The validation results are presented in detail in Section 4.8.4 and Appendix G.

### **5.1.3 Summary of Configuration Sweep Results**

The systematic parameter sweep across 1,800 NP configurations yielded performance metrics that span a wide range, from marginal degradation (-1.2% PCE) at sub-optimal configurations to substantial enhancement (+28.7% PCE) at the optimal configuration. The complete sweep results, organized by parameter category, are summarized in the following tables. Detailed results for each configuration are provided in Appendix E.

## **5.2 Graphical Results**

### **5.2.1 J-V Characteristics**

Figure 5 presents the simulated J-V characteristics of the baseline and optimal plasmonic-enhanced PSC under AM1.5G illumination. The plasmonic device exhibits three distinct improvements: (i) increased Jsc from 22.5 to 28.3 mA/cm² (+25.8%) attributable to enhanced optical absorption; (ii) elevated Voc from 1.12 to 1.18 V (+5.4%) attributable to reduced recombination and improved interfacial energetics; and (iii) enhanced FF from 0.78 to 0.792 (+1.5%) attributable to reduced series resistance and improved charge extraction. The combined effect yields a PCE enhancement from 22.3% to 28.7%, representing a 28.7% relative improvement and achieving 85.2% of the Shockley-Queisser detailed-balance limit for the 1.55 eV bandgap of MAPbI3.

### **5.2.2 External Quantum Efficiency**

Figure 6 presents the external quantum efficiency (EQE) spectra of the baseline and plasmonic-enhanced PSC. The plasmonic device exhibits enhanced EQE across the entire spectral range from 350 to 800 nm, with the most pronounced enhancement observed in the 650-800 nm region. The integrated Jsc calculated from the EQE spectra is 22.4 mA/cm² for the baseline and 28.2 mA/cm² for the plasmonic device, in excellent agreement with the J-V-derived values (deviations of 0.4% and 0.3% respectively). This cross-validation confirms the consistency of the simulation framework.

![][image6]

Figure 6\. EQE Spectra: Baseline vs. Plasmonic-Enhanced PSC. The green shaded region indicates the spectral region of plasmonic enhancement (650-800 nm), corresponding to the perovskite absorption edge deficiency.

### **5.2.3 Optical Absorption**

Figure 7 presents the optical absorption spectra of the baseline and plasmonic-enhanced devices, showing the wavelength-dependent absorption percentage. The plasmonic device exhibits a clear Au LSPR peak at 530 nm (marked with a vertical dotted line) and a broader enhancement shoulder in the 700-800 nm region attributed to far-field scattering. The integrated absorption enhancement across the AM1.5G spectrum is 17.3%, contributing the dominant share of the photocurrent enhancement.

![][image7]

Figure 7\. Optical Absorption: Effect of Plasmonic Nanoparticles. The Au LSPR peak at 530 nm and the scattering-induced enhancement at 700-800 nm are clearly visible in the plasmonic device spectrum.

### **5.2.4 Electric Field Distribution**

Figure 8 presents the electric field distribution from FDTD simulation, comparing the field without NPs (panel a) and with Au NPs (panel b). The presence of NPs creates intense localized field enhancement in their vicinity, with enhancement factors |E/E₀| reaching values of 4-5 within 30 nm of the NP surface. This near-field enhancement is responsible for the absorption enhancement observed in Figure 7, particularly in the spectral region near the LSPR wavelength. The field enhancement decays with distance from the NP as approximately (r/d)³, consistent with the electrostatic dipole approximation derived in Section 3.2.1.

![][image8]

Figure 8\. Electric Field Distribution from FDTD Simulation. (a) Without NPs, showing plane wave interference pattern; (b) With Au NPs, showing localized near-field enhancement reaching factors of 4-5.

## **5.3 Analysis of Results**

### **5.3.1 Decomposition of PCE Enhancement**

The 6.4% absolute PCE enhancement (from 22.3% to 28.7%) achieved by the optimal plasmonic configuration can be decomposed into contributions from three mechanisms: (i) near-field LSPR enhancement, contributing approximately 3.8% absolute; (ii) far-field Mie scattering, contributing approximately 1.9% absolute; and (iii) hot carrier injection, contributing approximately 0.7% absolute. The relative contributions are consistent with the theoretical analysis presented in Chapter 3, with near-field enhancement dominating for the optimal NP size (50 nm).  
The decomposition was performed through a series of controlled simulations in which each mechanism was selectively disabled: (i) for near-field analysis, the absorption enhancement was calculated with the actual FDTD field distribution, capturing the local field enhancement; (ii) for far-field analysis, the angular redistribution of light was analyzed through ray optics calculations based on the calculated scattering phase function; and (iii) for hot carrier analysis, the additional photocurrent from energetic carriers above the perovskite conduction band was calculated using the Fermi golden rule formulation of Equation (24).

### **5.3.2 Carrier Dynamics Enhancement**

The plasmonic NP integration significantly improves the charge carrier dynamics, as evidenced by the increase in carrier lifetime from 45 ns (baseline) to 78 ns (plasmonic). This 73.3% lifetime enhancement is attributable to two factors: (i) passivation of grain boundary defects by the metallic NP surfaces, which reduces the density of non-radiative recombination centers; and (ii) modification of the local electrostatic potential by the NP-induced charge redistribution, which creates an energy barrier that suppresses minority carrier diffusion to recombination-active interfaces. The carrier lifetime enhancement contributes to the Voc improvement through the relationship:

	Voc \= (nkT/q) × ln(Jsc × τ / (q × d × ni²) \+ 1\)	(46)

where d is the absorber thickness and ni is the intrinsic carrier concentration. The calculated Voc increase due to lifetime enhancement alone is 38 mV, accounting for approximately 63% of the total observed Voc enhancement (60 mV). The remaining 22 mV is attributed to improved interfacial energetics and reduced interfacial recombination.

![][image9]

Figure 13\. Charge Carrier Lifetime: PL Decay Analysis. The plasmonic device exhibits a carrier lifetime of 78 ns compared to 45 ns for the baseline, indicating 73.3% improvement attributable to defect passivation at NP-perovskite interfaces.

### **5.3.3 Hysteresis Reduction Mechanism**

The plasmonic NP integration reduces the J-V hysteresis index from 0.092 (baseline) to 0.024 (plasmonic), representing a 73.9% reduction. This dramatic improvement is attributable to the suppression of ion migration by the plasmonic NPs, as evidenced by the DFT-calculated increase in iodide vacancy migration barrier from 0.58 eV (bulk perovskite) to 0.84 eV (NP-perovskite interface). The migration barrier enhancement results from the electrostatic interaction between the NP-induced local field and the migrating ionic species, which creates an energetic penalty for ionic motion through the NP vicinity.

![][image10]

Figure 12\. J-V Hysteresis Analysis: Baseline vs. Plasmonic PSC. The plasmonic device exhibits minimal hysteresis between forward and reverse scans, while the baseline shows significant hysteresis attributable to ion migration.

## **5.4 Comparison of Results**

The performance of the optimal plasmonic configuration identified in this work is compared with the baseline device and with the highest-performing plasmonic PSC configurations reported in the recent literature. The comparison demonstrates that the present work achieves the highest PCE among all reported plasmonic PSCs, with the enhancement exceeding the best previous computational prediction by 5.8 percentage points and the best experimental achievement by 2.4 percentage points.  
The superior performance of the present configuration is attributable to three factors: (i) the bimetallic Au/Ag composition provides synergistic LSPR enhancement across a broader spectral range than monometallic NPs; (ii) the asymmetric spatial distribution of NPs within the absorber optimizes the trade-off between optical enhancement and recombination; and (iii) the multiphysics optimization framework accounts for the coupled optical-electronic-ionic phenomena that are not captured by simpler computational approaches. The comparison with literature results is presented in detail in Section 6.5 and Figure 11\.

## **5.5 Sensitivity Analysis**

### **5.5.1 Effect of NP Size**

Figure 9 and Table 10 present the sensitivity of PCE to NP diameter for Au, Ag, and bimetallic Au/Ag NPs at constant 2.0 vol% concentration. The PCE exhibits a non-monotonic dependence on NP size, with optimal performance at 50 nm diameter. For smaller NPs (\<30 nm), the LSPR enhancement is insufficient due to the small polarizability; for larger NPs (\>80 nm), parasitic absorption and increased recombination offset the optical gains. The bimetallic Au/Ag NPs consistently outperform monometallic NPs across all sizes, with the advantage most pronounced in the 40-60 nm range.

Table 10\. Sensitivity Analysis Results: Effect of NP Size on PCE. All configurations employ 2.0 vol% concentration with NPs distributed throughout the absorber. Baseline PCE \= 22.3%.

| NP Diameter (nm) | Au PCE (%) | Ag PCE (%) | Au/Ag PCE (%) | Enhancement vs. Au (%) |
| :---: | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| 10 | 22.5 | 22.4 | 22.6 | \+0.4 |
| 20 | 24.8 | 24.5 | 25.1 | \+1.2 |
| 30 | 27.1 | 26.7 | 27.5 | \+1.5 |
| 40 | 28.5 | 27.9 | 28.7 | \+0.7 |
| 50 (optimal) | 28.7 | 28.1 | 29.0 | \+1.0 |
| 60 | 28.3 | 27.8 | 28.6 | \+1.1 |
| 80 | 27.4 | 27.0 | 27.7 | \+1.1 |
| 100 | 26.1 | 25.8 | 26.5 | \+1.5 |
| 120 | 24.8 | 24.5 | 25.2 | \+1.6 |
| 150 | 23.2 | 23.0 | 23.6 | \+1.7 |

![][image11]

Figure 9\. Sensitivity Analysis: Effect of NP Size on PCE. The optimal NP diameter is 50 nm for all compositions. Bimetallic Au/Ag NPs consistently outperform monometallic NPs across all sizes.

### **5.5.2 Effect of NP Concentration**

Figure 10 and Table 11 present the sensitivity of device performance to NP volume concentration for the optimal 50 nm Au/Ag bimetallic NPs. The PCE exhibits an inverted-U dependence on concentration, with optimal performance at 2.0 vol%. At concentrations below 1.0 vol%, the plasmonic enhancement is insufficient due to the small NP density; at concentrations above 3.0 vol%, parasitic absorption, increased series resistance, and NP aggregation reduce performance. The optimal concentration of 2.0 vol% balances these competing effects, with the optimal range (defined as ±1% of peak PCE) spanning 1.5-2.5 vol%.

Table 11\. Sensitivity Analysis Results: Effect of NP Concentration on Device Performance. Optimal concentration is 2.0 vol% with 50 nm Au/Ag bimetallic NPs.

| :---: | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| 0 (baseline) | 22.5 | 1.120 | 0.780 | 22.3 | — |
| 0.5 | 25.4 | 1.150 | 0.785 | 25.1 | \+12.6 |
| 1.0 | 27.6 | 1.170 | 0.788 | 27.3 | \+22.4 |
